كتاب القسامة
أحكام القسامة
كتاب القسامة [سمي بها هذا الباب؛ لأن مبناه على الأيمان، وهي مشروعة فيه بالإجماع. (عيني)] لما كان أمر القتل يؤول إلى القسامة إذا لم يعرف قاتله شرع في بيانها للحاجة إليها والقسامة لغة: اسم ما وضع موضع الاقسام كذا في عامة الشروح أخذا من "المغرب"، وقال في "معراج الدراية : القسامة لغة مصدر أقسم كما لا يخفى ، دراية بعلم الأدب وشرعا هي . أيمان يقسم بها أهل محلة أو دار أو غير ذلك وجد فيها قتيل به أثر، يقول كل منهم: والله ما قتلت ولا علمت له قاتلا، ومن محاسن القسامة خلاص من يتهم بالقتل عن القصاص، وصيانة دم المقتول عن الإهدار بإيجاب الدية، وتعظيم أمر الدماء وخطرها بتكرار الأيمان. (فتح، تكملة) قتيل وجد إلخ أي إذا وجد قتيل في محلة ولم يدر ،قاتله حلف خمسون رجلا من أهل المحلة يتخيرهم الولي، فيحلفون بالله ما ،قتلناه ولا علمنا له ،قاتلا، ولا يحلف الولي، فإذا حلفوا قضى على أهل المحلة، يعني على عاقلتهم بالدية في ثلاث سنين سواء كانت دعوى القتل الخطأ أو العمد هذه هي صورة القسامة عندنا، وفيها خلاف للشافعي وغيره من الأئمة كما سيأتي. لم يدر :قاتله [ يجوز أن يكون حالا أو صفة بعد صفة. (مسكين)] إنما قيد به؛ لأنه لو علم كان القاتل هو الخصم، وتسقط القسامة، وعلم القاتل إما بإقراره أو بإقامة المدعي بينة. (فتح) حلف خمسون إلخ: سواء كانت الدعوى على جميع أهل المحلة، أو على بعض غير معين؛ لأن المدعى عليه لا يتميز عن الباقي، ولو ادعى على بعض معين عمدا أو خطأ فكذا الجواب، وإطلاق الكتاب يدل عليه، وعن أبي يوسف في رواية الأصول: أن القسامة والدية تسقط عن الباقين من أهل المحلة، ويقال للولي : ألك بينة؟ فإن قال: لا، يستحلف المدعى عليه يمينا واحدا، وروى ابن المبارك عن أ أبي حنيفة مثله. (فتح، عيني) يتخيرهم الولي: أي يتخير الخمسين ولي القتيل؛ لأن اليمين حقه، فيكون التعيين إليه، والظاهر أنه يختار من يتهمه الفسقة والشبان، أو صالحي أهل المحلة؛ لأن تحرزهم عن اليمين الكاذبة أبلغ، فيظهر القاتل. (فتح) بالله : ه متعلق بقوله: "حلف" أي حلف خمسون رجلا بالله. (عيني) بالقتل، وهم بالله ما ما قتلناه ولا علمنا إلخ : هذا على سبيل الحكاية عن الجميع، وأما عند الحلف، فيحلف كل واحد منهم قتلته، ولا علمت له قاتلا؛ لجواز أنه قتله وحده، فيجترئ علي يمينه بالله ما قتلناه يعني جميعا، ولا يعكس؛ لأنه إذا غيره كان قاتلا له [ رمز الحقائق: ٢٤٤/٩] فإن قيل : أي فائدة في قوله: ما علمت له قاتلا . قتله أهل المحلة بالقتل غير مقبولة؛ لأنهم يسقطون الضمان عن أنفسهم؟ قلنا: فائدته تعيين محل الخصومة، فإن الولي - مع. مع أن شهادة = فإن حلفوا فعلى أهل المحلة الدية ولا يحلف الولي، وحبس الآبي حتى يحلف، عاقلة أهل المحلة في ثلاث سنين قد يعجز عن تعيينه، ويظن غير القاتل قاتلا، ويجوز أن تكون الفائدة هي أن يقر الحالف على عبده، فيقبل إقراره، أو يقر على عبد غيره من غير أهل المحلة فيصدقه ولي المقتول فيسقط الحكم عن أهل المحلة. (فتح، تكملة) فإن حلفوا إلخ: ومن نكل منهم حبس حتى يحلف أو يقر أو يموت جوعا، وهذا إذا ادعى الولي قتل العمد، أما لو ادعى قتل الخطأ، فالقضاء بالدية عند النكول. (فتح) ولا يحلف الولي مطلقا سواء كان هناك لوث أولا] أي لا يحلف ولي القتيل عندنا وإن وجد اللوث، وقال الشافعي: إذا كان هناك لوث استحلف الأولياء خمسين يمينا ويقضى لهم بالدية على المدعى عليه عمدا كانت الدعوى أو خطأ، وهو قوله الصحيح، وفي القديم: إذا حلف أنهم قتلوه عمدا فله القصاص، وبه قال مالك وأحمد، وإن نكل الأولياء عن اليمين استحلف أهل المحلة، فإن حلفوا برءوا، وإن نكلوا حكم عليهم بالدية. الشافعي في موضعين أحدهما أن المدعي لا يحلف عندنا خلافا له، والثاني: في براءة أهل المحلة باليمين، واللوث قرينة حالية توقع في القلب صدق المدعي بأن يكون هناك علامة القتل على واحد بعينه، أو ظاهر يشهد للمدعي من عداوة ظاهرة، أو شهادة عدل، أو جماعة غير عدول أن أهل المحلة قتلوه، وإنما شرط اللوث؛ لأن مذهبه عند عدمه كمذهبنا لهم ما روي أن عبد الله بن سهل وجد قتيلا في قليب من قلب خيبر، فقال عمر: رسول الله ! إنا وجدنا عبد الله بن سهل قتيلا في قليب من قلب خيبر وذكر عداوة يهود لهم، فقال: "أفتبرئكم يهود بخمسين يمينا أنهم لم يقتلوه"، قال: قلت: فكيف نرضى بأيمانهم وهم مشركون؟ قال: "فيقسم منكم خمسون أنهم قتلوه"، قالوا: كيف نقسم على ما لم نر، فوداه رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم من عنده. فالخلاف وارد من ولنا قوله عليها: "لو أعطى الناس بدعواهم لادعى ناس دماء ناس وأموالهم ولكن البينة على المدعي، واليمين على من أنكر"، فسوى في ذلك بين الدماء والأموال وحكم فيهما بحكم واحد، وما رووه ضعفه جماعة أهل الحديث، فلا يلزم حجة، ولئن ثبت إنما قال ذلك على سبيل الاستفهام؛ إنكارا عليهم لما لم يرضوا بأيمانهم، فكأنه قال لهم: إن اليهود وإن كانوا كفارا ليس عليهم فيما تدعون عليهم غير أيمانهم، وكما لا يقبل منكم وإن كنتم مسلمين ،أيمانكم فتستحقون بها، كذلك لا يجب على اليهود بدعواكم عليهم غير أيمانهم، والدليل على صحة هذا التأويل حكم عمر الله به بعد النبي عليه بمحضر من الصحابة من غير إنكار أحد منهم، فصار إجماعا. (عيني) وحبس الآبي إلخ: يعني من أبي منهم من الحلف حبس حتى يحلف أو يقر، فيلزمه ما أقربه، وإنما لم يحكم بمجرد النكول؛ لأن اليمين هنا نفس الحق تعظيما لأمر الدم لا بدل عن الدية، ولذا يجمع بينهما، بخلاف اليمين في دعوى المال؛ لأنها بدل عنه، ولهذا تسقط بالأداء. واعلم أن قوله : وحبس" الآبي حتى يحلف" لم يأخذه أحد شراح "الكنز" لكن لما كان في النسخ المطبوعة هكذا أبقيته على حاله
The Book of Qasamah
Rulings of Qasamah
The Book of Qasamah [This chapter is named because it is based on oaths, and it is legislated by consensus. (Ayni)] Since the matter of killing leads to qasamah when the killer is unknown, its explanation is begun due to need. Qasamah linguistically: a name for what is placed in the position of oaths, as in the general commentaries taken from al-Mughrib. It is said in Mirqat al-Dirayah: Qasamah linguistically is a verbal noun of aqsama, as is not hidden, with knowledge of literature. Legally: it is oaths sworn by the people of a locality or a house or other place where a slain person with traces is found, each of them saying: By Allah, I did not kill him nor do I know a killer for him. Among the excellences of qasamah is the deliverance of one accused of killing from retaliation, the protection of the blood of the slain from being squandered by imposing blood money, and the magnifying of the matter of blood and its danger by repeating oaths. (Fath, Takmilah) 'A slain person found, etc.'—i.e., if a slain person is found in a locality and his killer is not known, fifty men from the people of the locality swear, chosen by the guardian (wali). They swear by Allah: 'We did not kill him nor do we know a killer for him.' The guardian does not swear. If they swear, judgment is given against the people of the locality, meaning against their 'aqilah (tribal guarantors), for blood money payable in three years, whether the claim is for accidental or intentional killing. This is the form of qasamah according to us. There is a difference of opinion from al-Shafi'i and other imams, as will come. 'Did not know his killer'—[it can be a circumstantial accusative or an adjective after an adjective. (Miskin)] It is only restricted by this because if the killer were known, he would be the opponent, and qasamah would drop. Knowledge of the killer is either by his confession or by the plaintiff establishing evidence. (Fath) 'Fifty swear, etc.': Whether the claim is against all the people of the locality or against some unspecified individuals, because the accused is not distinguished from the rest. If the claim is against a specific individual for intentional or accidental killing, the answer is likewise, and the absolute wording of the book indicates it. From Abu Yusuf in Riwayat al-Usul: Qasamah and blood money drop from the rest of the people of the locality, and it is said to the guardian: 'Do you have evidence?' If he says no, the accused is made to swear one oath. Ibn al-Mubarak narrated from Abu Hanifa the like. (Fath, Ayni) 'The guardian chooses them': i.e., the guardian of the slain chooses the fifty because the oath is his right, so the designation is up to him. Apparently he chooses those he suspects—the sinners and youths, or the righteous of the locality, because their caution against false oath is greater, so the killer may be revealed. (Fath) 'By Allah'—this is related to 'they swear', i.e., fifty men swear by Allah. (Ayni) 'We did not kill him nor do we know a killer, etc.': This is in the manner of narration from all. As for when swearing, each one of them swears: 'I did not kill him nor do I know a killer for him'—because it is possible he killed him alone, so he would dare to swear 'We did not kill him' meaning all together, and it is not the reverse because if someone else killed him, then... [Reference: Ramz al-Haqa'iq 244/9] If it is said: What benefit is there in his saying 'I do not know a killer for him' since the testimony of the people of the locality about the killing is not admissible because they are removing liability from themselves? We say: Its benefit is to specify the place of dispute, because the guardian may be unable to specify and may think a non-killer is the killer. It is also possible that the benefit is that the swearer confesses against his own slave, and his confession is accepted, or he confesses against another's slave from outside the locality, and the guardian of the slain believes him, so the judgment drops from the people of the locality. (Fath, Takmilah) 'If they swear, etc.': Whoever among them refuses is imprisoned until he swears or confesses or dies of hunger. This is if the guardian claims intentional killing. As for if he claims accidental killing, then judgment is given for blood money upon refusal. (Fath) 'And the guardian does not swear' absolutely, whether there is circumstantial evidence (lawth) or not—i.e., the guardian of the slain does not swear according to us, even if lawth exists. Al-Shafi'i said: If there is lawth, the guardians swear fifty oaths and judgment is given for them of blood money against the accused, whether the claim is intentional or accidental—this is his correct saying. In his old opinion: If they swear that they killed him intentionally, then he has retaliation. This is also the view of Malik and Ahmad. If the guardians refuse the oath, then the people of the locality are made to swear; if they swear, they are cleared; if they refuse, judgment is given against them for blood money. The difference is in two places: one, that the plaintiff does not swear according to us, contrary to him; the second, in the clearing of the people of the locality by oath. And lawth is a circumstantial indication that instills in the heart the truthfulness of the plaintiff, such as there being a sign of killing on a specific person, or an apparent indication from open enmity, or the testimony of a just person, or a group of non-just persons that the people of the locality killed him. He (Shafi'i) only required lawth because his view in its absence is like our view. Their evidence is what is narrated that Abdullah ibn Sahl was found killed in a well of the wells of Khaybar. Umar said: O Messenger of Allah! We found Abdullah ibn Sahl killed in a well of the wells of Khaybar, and he mentioned the enmity of the Jews towards them. He said: 'Would the Jews clear themselves by fifty oaths that they did not kill him?' He said: I said: How can we be satisfied with their oaths while they are polytheists? He said: 'Then fifty of you should swear that they killed him.' They said: How can we swear over what we did not see? So the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) paid the blood money from his own wealth. Our evidence is His saying (peace be upon him): 'If people were given according to their claims, they would claim the blood and property of people, but the burden of proof is on the claimant and the oath is on the denier.' So he equated blood and property and ruled in both with a single ruling. What they narrated was declared weak by a group of hadith scholars, so it is not binding proof. Even if it is established, he said that by way of inquiry, disapproving of them when they were not satisfied with their oaths, as if he said to them: The Jews, even if they are disbelievers, have nothing against them in what you claim but their oaths, and just as your oaths are not accepted from you even though you are Muslims to deserve anything, so it is not necessary upon the Jews by your claim against them except their oaths. The evidence for the correctness of this interpretation is the judgment of Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) after the Prophet (peace be upon him) in the presence of the Companions without any of them disapproving, so it became consensus. (Ayni) 'And the imprisonment of the one who refuses, etc.': i.e., whoever among them refuses to swear is imprisoned until he swears or confesses, and he is obliged by what he confesses. Judgment is not executed by mere refusal because the oath here is the right itself to magnify the matter of blood, not a substitute for blood money, so they are combined. Unlike the oath in claims of property because it is a substitute for it, so it drops upon payment. Know that the saying 'and the imprisonment of the one who refuses until he swears' was not taken by any of the commentators of al-Kanz, but since it is in the printed copies thus, I have left it as it is.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the Hanafi rules of qasamah—the oaths taken when a corpse is found and the killer is unknown. The author begins by defining qasamah linguistically and legally, emphasizing that it is a legislated institution based on oaths. The core ruling: if a slain person is found in a locality, fifty men from that locality are selected by the guardian (wali) of the deceased. They swear by Allah that they neither killed him nor know his killer. If they swear, the blood money (diyah) is imposed on the 'aqilah (tribal guarantors) of that locality, payable over three years, whether the claim is for intentional or accidental killing. The guardian himself does not swear. This is the Hanafi position, and the text notes a difference with Imam al-Shafi'i and others. The commentary then addresses nuances: the condition that the killer is unknown (if known, qasamah drops); whether the claim is against all or some; a narration from Abu Yusuf about dropping qasamah for the rest if the accused is specific; the guardian's choice of fifty men (he chooses those he suspects or the righteous to maximize truthfulness); the wording of the oath (each individual swears in the singular, not plural, because if he alone killed, he would be lying in the plural). The text then answers the objection: what benefit is there in saying 'I do not know a killer' since their testimony about killing is not accepted? The benefit is to specify the dispute, and it can also allow confession against a slave. Then it states that if they refuse to swear, they are imprisoned until they swear or confess (for intentional killing claim). For accidental claim, judgment for blood money is given upon refusal. The major dispute with Shafi'i: According to us, the guardian never swears, even if there is lawth (strong circumstantial evidence). Shafi'i holds that if there is lawth, the guardians swear fifty oaths and get blood money (or in his old view, retaliation if they swear intentional killing). If the guardians refuse, the locality swears to clear themselves. The Hanafi proof is the hadith 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, the oath on the denier' which equates blood with property. The Shafi'i proof is the hadith of Abdullah ibn Sahl, but the Hanafis respond that this hadith is weak, and even if sound, the Prophet's statement was an inquiry disapproving their refusal of Jewish oaths, not a ruling. Moreover, Umar's subsequent judgment in the presence of Companions without objection establishes consensus. Finally, the imprisonment of the one who refuses: the oath itself is the right, not a substitute for blood money, so refusal doesn't automatically impose liability; imprisonment continues until oath or confession.

Key Terms
قسامة (qasamah): In this text, qasamah refers to the oaths sworn by fifty men from a locality where a corpse is found, declaring they did not kill the deceased nor know the killer.
ولي (wali): The guardian of the slain person, who has the right to seek justice or blood money. He selects the fifty men to swear but does not swear himself.
لوث (lawth): Strong circumstantial evidence that instills a strong belief in the truthfulness of the claimant, such as open enmity or a witness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the effect of lawth on qasamah?
A) The guardian must swear
B) The locality's men must swear more than fifty oaths
C) The ruling remains the same: the locality's men swear, guardian does not
D) The case is dismissed

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the guardian never swears in qasamah, even with strong circumstantial evidence (lawth); the locality's men bear the oath.

The Plaintiff Never Swears: Qasamah Rule and the Shafi'i Dispute
Rulings on qasamah procedure and dispute with Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that the plaintiff does not swear in qasamah, cite the primary evidence, and contrast it with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
In qasamah, the plaintiff never swears; the accused district swears to exonerate or pays diya.

This lesson focuses on a core rule in the law of qasamah: the burden of oath. According to the Hanafi school, when there is strong circumstantial evidence (lawth) in a homicide case, the guardian of the victim does not swear. Instead, the residents of the district where the body is found swear fifty oaths to deny involvement; if they refuse, they become liable for the blood-money (diya). This ruling is based on general hadith principles and the practice of the Companions, and it directly opposes the view of Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Ruling
Identify that the wali (guardian) does not swear at all, and the accused district swears fifty oaths. If they refuse, they pay diya.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the Opposing School
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that if there is lawth, the wali swears fifty oaths and receives diya. If the wali refuses, the accused swears.
Also held by Malik and Ahmad in one view.
3
Trace the Hanafi Dalil
The hadith 'لو أعطى الناس بدعواهم...' places the oath on the denier, not the claimant. Umar's practice with Companions' consensus confirms this.
The hadith is considered qat'i al-thubut but zanni al-dalala in application.
4
Analyse the Shafi'i Proof
They rely on the hadith of Abdullah ibn Sahl where the Prophet suggested the guardians swear. The Hanafi response critiques its authenticity and interpretation.
The Hanafis argue it was a rhetorical question, not a command.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih
The Hanafi position is preferred due to stronger textual evidence and Companions' consensus.
This is the relied-upon view for fatwa.

كتاب القسامة
أحكام القسامة
فإن حلفوا فعلى أهل المحلة الدية ولا يحلف الولي، وحبس الآبي حتى يحلف، عاقلة أهل المحلة في ثلاث سنين
قد يعجز عن تعيينه، ويظن غير القاتل قاتلا، ويجوز أن تكون الفائدة هي أن يقر الحالف على عبده، فيقبل إقراره، أو يقر على عبد غيره من غير أهل المحلة فيصدقه ولي المقتول فيسقط الحكم عن أهل المحلة. (فتح، تكملة) فإن حلفوا إلخ: ومن نكل منهم حبس حتى يحلف أو يقر أو يموت جوعا، وهذا إذا ادعى الولي قتل العمد، أما لو ادعى قتل الخطأ، فالقضاء بالدية عند النكول. (فتح) ولا يحلف الولي مطلقا سواء كان هناك لوث أولا] أي لا يحلف ولي القتيل عندنا وإن وجد اللوث، وقال الشافعي: إذا كان هناك لوث استحلف الأولياء خمسين يمينا ويقضى لهم بالدية على المدعى عليه عمدا كانت الدعوى أو خطأ، وهو قوله الصحيح، وفي القديم: إذا حلف أنهم قتلوه عمدا فله القصاص، وبه قال مالك وأحمد، وإن نكل الأولياء عن اليمين استحلف أهل المحلة، فإن حلفوا برءوا، وإن نكلوا حكم عليهم بالدية. الشافعي في موضعين أحدهما أن المدعي لا يحلف عندنا خلافا له، والثاني: في براءة أهل المحلة باليمين، واللوث قرينة حالية توقع في القلب صدق المدعي بأن يكون هناك علامة القتل على واحد بعينه، أو ظاهر يشهد للمدعي من عداوة ظاهرة، أو شهادة عدل، أو جماعة غير عدول أن أهل المحلة قتلوه، وإنما شرط اللوث؛ لأن مذهبه عند عدمه كمذهبنا لهم ما روي أن عبد الله بن سهل وجد قتيلا في قليب من قلب خيبر، فقال عمر: رسول الله ! إنا وجدنا عبد الله بن سهل قتيلا في قليب من قلب خيبر وذكر عداوة يهود لهم، فقال: "أفتبرئكم يهود بخمسين يمينا أنهم لم يقتلوه"، قال: قلت: فكيف نرضى بأيمانهم وهم مشركون؟ قال: "فيقسم منكم خمسون أنهم قتلوه"، قالوا: كيف نقسم على ما لم نر، فوداه رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم من عنده.

وإن لم يتم إلخ: يعني إن لم يتم عدد أهل المحلة بل نقصوا عن خمسين كرر الحلف عليهم حتى يتم خمسون يمينا، فإن كان واحد حلف خمسين يمينا وذلك لأن الخمسين وجبت بالنص، فيجب تمامه ما أمكن، ولا يشترط فيه الوقوف على الفائدة فيما يثبت بالنص، ولأن عمر له لما قضى في القسامة وافى إليه تسعة وأربعون رجلا، فكرر اليمين على رجل منهم حتى تمت خمسين، ثم قضى بالدية، وعن شريح والنخعي ما مثل ذلك، وإن كان العدد كاملا، فأراد الولي أن يكرر على أحدهم ليس له ذلك؛ لأن المصير إلى التكرار ضرورة الإكمال، وقد كمل. تكملة هداية ولا قسامة على صبي إلخ لأنهم ليسوا من أهل النصرة وإنما هم أتباع، والنصرة لا تقوم بالأتباع، واليمين على أهل النصرة؛ ولأن الصبي والمجنون ليسا من أهل القول الصحيح، واليمين قول.

[رمز الحقائق: ٤٦٧/٢] =
Book of Qasamah
Rulings of Qasamah
If they swear, then the residents of the district (mahalla) are liable for the blood-money (diya), and the guardian (wali) does not swear. And the one who refuses is imprisoned until he swears. The blood-money is payable by the 'aqila (responsible clan) of the district in three years.
He may be unable to identify him, and he may suspect someone other than the killer as the killer. And it is permissible that the benefit is that the swearer confesses against his own slave, so his confession is accepted, or he confesses against another's slave not from the district, and the guardian of the slain believes him, so judgment against the district falls. (Fath, Takmila) 'If they swear, etc.': And whoever among them refrains from swearing is imprisoned until he swears or confesses, or dies of hunger. This is if the guardian claims intentional killing; but if he claims unintentional killing, then judgment for diya is given upon refusal. (Fath) And the guardian does not swear at all, whether there is lawth (circumstantial evidence) or not. That is, the guardian of the slain does not swear according to us even if there is lawth. Al-Shafi'i said: If there is lawth, the guardians are made to swear fifty oaths, and judgment is given for them of diya against the accused, whether the claim is intentional or unintentional—that is his correct opinion. In the old opinion: if they swear that they killed him intentionally, then he has the right to retaliation (qisas). Malik and Ahmad said the same. If the guardians refrain from the oath, then the residents of the district are made to swear; if they swear, they are exonerated; if they refrain, judgment of diya is given against them. Al-Shafi'i (differs) in two places: one, the plaintiff does not swear according to us, contrary to him; the second, the exoneration of the residents by oath. Lawth is a circumstantial clue that causes the heart to believe the plaintiff, such as there being a mark of killing on a specific person, or an apparent evidence for the plaintiff from manifest enmity, or the testimony of a just person, or a group of non-just persons that the residents of the district killed him. Lawth is required only because his school in its absence is like our school. They narrated that Abdullah ibn Sahl was found killed in a well of the wells of Khaybar. Umar said: O Messenger of Allah! We found Abdullah ibn Sahl killed in a well of the wells of Khaybar, and he mentioned the enmity of the Jews towards them. He said: "Would the Jews exonerate you with fifty oaths that they did not kill him?" He said: I said: How can we be satisfied with their oaths while they are polytheists? He said: "Then fifty of you should take an oath that they killed him." They said: How can we take an oath on what we did not see? So the Messenger of Allah paid blood-money (diya) from his own side.
So the dispute...
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Qasamah (oaths in homicide) covers several rulings. It begins with the rule: if the residents of the district where the body is found swear (i.e., take an oath of innocence collectively), then they pay the diya collectively to the guardian of the slain. The guardian himself does not swear. If someone refuses to swear, he is imprisoned until he does. The diya is payable over three years by the 'aqila (the clan responsible for blood-money). The text then explains that the plaintiff (wali) does not swear at all according to the Hanafi school, whether there is lawth (strong circumstantial evidence) or not. This is a major point of disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i. The Shafi'i position: if lawth exists, the guardians of the slain are made to swear fifty oaths, and then diya is awarded; in one old opinion, they could claim qisas (retaliation). If the guardians refuse, the accused residents swear to exonerate themselves; if they refuse, they are liable for diya. The Hanafi rebuttal is based on the general hadith: 'If people were given according to their claims, they would claim the blood and property of people, but the burden of proof (bayyinah) is on the claimant, and the oath (yamin) is on the one who denies.' This hadith treats blood and property alike, placing the oath on the one who denies (the accused), not the plaintiff. The hadith cited by the opponent (concerning Abdullah ibn Sahl) is considered weak by hadith scholars; and even if authentic, it is interpreted as a rhetorical objection by the Prophet to the guardians' reluctance to accept the Jews' oaths. The practice of Umar after the Prophet, with the Companions' consensus, supports the Hanafi position. The passage then addresses the number of oaths: if the number of male residents is less than fifty, the oaths are repeated on them until fifty oaths are completed. Even if only one man is present, he swears fifty oaths, because the number fifty is established by text (nass) and must be realized. This is supported by the precedent of Umar who had forty-nine men, so he repeated the oath on one man. If the number is complete, the guardian cannot force repetition. Next, the text lists those who do not participate in qasamah: a minor, an insane person, a woman, and a slave. They are not considered 'people of support' (ahl al-nusrah); they are followers, and support (nusrah) is not established by followers. Oaths are on those capable of providing support. Also, minors and insane persons cannot give valid speech (qawl), and the oath is a speech act. Then, the text enumerates cases where neither qasamah nor diya apply: a corpse with no external mark of violence (i.e., no wound, strangulation, or similar), or if blood flows from the nose, mouth, or anus. These are natural exits for blood, so they do not indicate homicide. Exception: if blood flows from the eye or ear, qasamah and diya apply, because blood from these places normally indicates a blow. Finally, the passage rules on a corpse found on an animal with a driver (sa'iq), leader (qa'id), or rider: the diya is on the 'aqila of the driver, leader, or rider, not on the residents of the district, because the deceased was under their control. If all three are present, they share the diya equally. They need not own the animal, unlike the case of a house (where ownership may matter). Notes from later commentators (Fath, 'Ayni, Ramz al-Haqa'iq) add details on the required part of the body found, and on the presence of a fetus. The overall theme is limiting the application of qasamah to specific conditions and identifying the proper parties liable.

Key Terms
القسامة (al-qasāmah): A special legal procedure in homicide cases involving multiple oaths to determine liability for blood-money when there is strong circumstantial evidence but no direct proof.
اللوث (al-lawth): Strong circumstantial evidence that suggests the truth of the plaintiff's claim, such as a clear sign of murder on the body, enmity, or testimony of a just person or group.
العاقلة (al-ʿāqilah): The clan or group of male relatives responsible for paying blood-money (diya) on behalf of a killer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling when there is lawth in a qasamah case?
A) The wali (guardian) swears fifty oaths to prove the claim.
B) The residents of the district swear fifty oaths to exonerate themselves.
C) Both the wali and residents swear fifty oaths each.
D) No oaths are taken; the case is decided by evidence.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school of qasamah, the burden of oath lies on the accused district, not the plaintiff, based on the general rule that the oath is on the denier.

Conditions and Exclusions in Qasamah: Number of Oaths, Exempt Persons, and Liability on Animal Handlers
Rulings on number of oaths, exempt persons, and liability on animal handlers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the number of oaths required, identify who is exempt from qasamah, and state the liability when a body is found on an animal.

Core Idea
Qasamah requires fifty oaths even if repeated on one person; minors, women, etc., do not participate; without visible violence, no qasamah.

This lesson covers the practical details of the qasamah procedure. The passage explains that if the male residents are fewer than fifty, oaths are repeated until fifty are completed. It then lists those who are not eligible to participate: minors, insane persons, women, and slaves. Finally, it addresses the special case of a body found on an animal being led by a driver, leader, or rider – in that case, the liability falls on the handlers' 'aqila, not the residents.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Requirement of Fifty Oaths
The number fifty is based on nass (text). If the male residents are less than fifty, oaths are repeated on them until the count is complete, even if one man swears fifty times.
This is from the precedent of Umar with forty-nine men.
2
Identify Exempt Persons
Minors, insane persons, women, and slaves are not eligible to swear because they are not considered 'ahl al-nusrah' (people of support); they are followers. Also, minors and insane persons cannot give valid speech (qawl).
The oath is a speech act requiring legal capacity.
3
Recognize Conditions for Qasamah
Qasamah and diya are not imposed if the corpse shows no external mark of violence (wound, strangulation, etc.) or if blood flows from natural exits like nose, mouth, or anus. Exception: blood from eye or ear indicates a blow.
The presence of a clear sign of homicide is essential.
4
Apply the Rule for Body on an Animal
If the found body is on an animal with a driver (sa'iq), leader (qa'id), or rider, the diya is on the 'aqila of the handler(s), not the residents. If all three are present, they share equally.
The deceased was under their control, similar to being in their house.

ولنا قوله عليها: "لو أعطى الناس بدعواهم لادعى ناس دماء ناس وأموالهم ولكن البينة على المدعي، واليمين على من أنكر"، فسوى في ذلك بين الدماء والأموال وحكم فيهما بحكم واحد، وما رووه ضعفه جماعة أهل الحديث، فلا يلزم حجة، ولئن ثبت إنما قال ذلك على سبيل الاستفهام؛ إنكارا عليهم لما لم يرضوا بأيمانهم، فكأنه قال لهم: إن اليهود وإن كانوا كفارا ليس عليهم فيما تدعون عليهم غير أيمانهم، وكما لا يقبل منكم وإن كنتم مسلمين ،أيمانكم فتستحقون بها، كذلك لا يجب على اليهود بدعواكم عليهم غير أيمانهم، والدليل على صحة هذا التأويل حكم عمر الله به بعد النبي عليه بمحضر من الصحابة من غير إنكار أحد منهم، فصار إجماعا. كتاب القسامة
ចុះ ។
أحكام القسامة
وإن لم يتم العدد، كرر الحلف عليهم ليتم خمسون ولا قسامة على صبي ومجنون وامرأة
أي عدد خمسين
يميتا
وعبد، ولا قسامة ولا دية في ميت لا أثر به أو يسيل دم من أنفه أو فمه أو دبره،
من جرح ونحوه
بخلاف عينه وأذنه. قتيل على دابة معها سائق أو قائد أو راكب، فديته على عاقلته. شرونکی کشونکی
السائق
وإن لم يتم إلخ: يعني إن لم يتم عدد أهل المحلة بل نقصوا عن خمسين كرر الحلف عليهم حتى يتم خمسون يمينا، فإن كان واحد حلف خمسين يمينا وذلك لأن الخمسين وجبت بالنص، فيجب تمامه ما أمكن، ولا يشترط فيه الوقوف على الفائدة فيما يثبت بالنص، ولأن عمر له لما قضى في القسامة وافى إليه تسعة وأربعون رجلا، فكرر اليمين على رجل منهم حتى تمت خمسين، ثم قضى بالدية، وعن شريح والنخعي ما مثل ذلك، وإن كان العدد كاملا، فأراد الولي أن يكرر على أحدهم ليس له ذلك؛ لأن المصير إلى التكرار ضرورة الإكمال، وقد كمل. تكملة هداية ولا قسامة على صبي إلخ لأنهم ليسوا من أهل النصرة وإنما هم أتباع، والنصرة لا تقوم بالأتباع، واليمين على أهل النصرة؛ ولأن الصبي والمجنون ليسا من أهل القول الصحيح، واليمين قول.

الأثر
ولا قسامة ولا دية إلخ لان القسامة تجب في القتيل، وهذا ليس بقتيل، وإنما مات حتف أنفه، فلابد من أثر يكون بالميت يستدل به على أنه قتيل، وذلك بأن يكون به جراحة أو أثر ضرب أو خنق، فإذا لم يكن به شيء من لا يكون بفعل البشر، فلا يكون قتيلا، وكذا إذا خرج الدم من فيه أو ذكره أو دبره؛ لأن هذه المخارج يخرج منها الدم عادة، فلا يستدل به على أنه قتيل رمز الحقائق: ٤٦٧/٢] وقال في "الفتح: واعلم أن خروج الدم من المواضع مقيد بما إذا وجد من غير ضرب كذا في "الشرنبلالية" عن "الخانية"، "، وبقي قيد آخر فيما إذا خرج الدم من عليه في "الشرنبلالية" أيضا، وهو أن يكون نازلا من رأسه، فإن كان يعلو من جوفه . يكون قتيلا.

[رمز الحقائق: ٤٦٧/٢] =
Book of Qasamah
Rulings of Qasamah
If they swear, then the residents of the district (mahalla) are liable for the blood-money (diya), and the guardian (wali) does not swear. And the one who refuses is imprisoned until he swears. The blood-money is payable by the 'aqila (responsible clan) of the district in three years.
He may be unable to identify him, and he may suspect someone other than the killer as the killer. And it is permissible that the benefit is that the swearer confesses against his own slave, so his confession is accepted, or he confesses against another's slave not from the district, and the guardian of the slain believes him, so judgment against the district falls. (Fath, Takmila) 'If they swear, etc.': And whoever among them refrains from swearing is imprisoned until he swears or confesses, or dies of hunger. This is if the guardian claims intentional killing; but if he claims unintentional killing, then judgment for diya is given upon refusal. (Fath) And the guardian does not swear at all, whether there is lawth (circumstantial evidence) or not. That is, the guardian of the slain does not swear according to us even if there is lawth. Al-Shafi'i said: If there is lawth, the guardians are made to swear fifty oaths, and judgment is given for them of diya against the accused, whether the claim is intentional or unintentional—that is his correct opinion. In the old opinion: if they swear that they killed him intentionally, then he has the right to retaliation (qisas). Malik and Ahmad said the same. If the guardians refrain from the oath, then the residents of the district are made to swear; if they swear, they are exonerated; if they refrain, judgment of diya is given against them. Al-Shafi'i (differs) in two places: one, the plaintiff does not swear according to us, contrary to him; the second, the exoneration of the residents by oath. Lawth is a circumstantial clue that causes the heart to believe the plaintiff, such as there being a mark of killing on a specific person, or an apparent evidence for the plaintiff from manifest enmity, or the testimony of a just person, or a group of non-just persons that the residents of the district killed him. Lawth is required only because his school in its absence is like our school. They narrated that Abdullah ibn Sahl was found killed in a well of the wells of Khaybar. Umar said: O Messenger of Allah! We found Abdullah ibn Sahl killed in a well of the wells of Khaybar, and he mentioned the enmity of the Jews towards them. He said: "Would the Jews exonerate you with fifty oaths that they did not kill him?" He said: I said: How can we be satisfied with their oaths while they are polytheists? He said: "Then fifty of you should take an oath that they killed him." They said: How can we take an oath on what we did not see? So the Messenger of Allah paid blood-money (diya) from his own side.
So the dispute...
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Qasamah (oaths in homicide) covers several rulings. It begins with the rule: if the residents of the district where the body is found swear (i.e., take an oath of innocence collectively), then they pay the diya collectively to the guardian of the slain. The guardian himself does not swear. If someone refuses to swear, he is imprisoned until he does. The diya is payable over three years by the 'aqila (the clan responsible for blood-money). The text then explains that the plaintiff (wali) does not swear at all according to the Hanafi school, whether there is lawth (strong circumstantial evidence) or not. This is a major point of disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i. The Shafi'i position: if lawth exists, the guardians of the slain are made to swear fifty oaths, and then diya is awarded; in one old opinion, they could claim qisas (retaliation). If the guardians refuse, the accused residents swear to exonerate themselves; if they refuse, they are liable for diya. The Hanafi rebuttal is based on the general hadith: 'If people were given according to their claims, they would claim the blood and property of people, but the burden of proof (bayyinah) is on the claimant, and the oath (yamin) is on the one who denies.' This hadith treats blood and property alike, placing the oath on the one who denies (the accused), not the plaintiff. The hadith cited by the opponent (concerning Abdullah ibn Sahl) is considered weak by hadith scholars; and even if authentic, it is interpreted as a rhetorical objection by the Prophet to the guardians' reluctance to accept the Jews' oaths. The practice of Umar after the Prophet, with the Companions' consensus, supports the Hanafi position. The passage then addresses the number of oaths: if the number of male residents is less than fifty, the oaths are repeated on them until fifty oaths are completed. Even if only one man is present, he swears fifty oaths, because the number fifty is established by text (nass) and must be realized. This is supported by the precedent of Umar who had forty-nine men, so he repeated the oath on one man. If the number is complete, the guardian cannot force repetition. Next, the text lists those who do not participate in qasamah: a minor, an insane person, a woman, and a slave. They are not considered 'people of support' (ahl al-nusrah); they are followers, and support (nusrah) is not established by followers. Oaths are on those capable of providing support. Also, minors and insane persons cannot give valid speech (qawl), and the oath is a speech act. Then, the text enumerates cases where neither qasamah nor diya apply: a corpse with no external mark of violence (i.e., no wound, strangulation, or similar), or if blood flows from the nose, mouth, or anus. These are natural exits for blood, so they do not indicate homicide. Exception: if blood flows from the eye or ear, qasamah and diya apply, because blood from these places normally indicates a blow. Finally, the passage rules on a corpse found on an animal with a driver (sa'iq), leader (qa'id), or rider: the diya is on the 'aqila of the driver, leader, or rider, not on the residents of the district, because the deceased was under their control. If all three are present, they share the diya equally. They need not own the animal, unlike the case of a house (where ownership may matter). Notes from later commentators (Fath, 'Ayni, Ramz al-Haqa'iq) add details on the required part of the body found, and on the presence of a fetus. The overall theme is limiting the application of qasamah to specific conditions and identifying the proper parties liable.

Key Terms
القسامة (al-qasāmah): A special legal procedure in homicide cases involving multiple oaths to determine liability for blood-money when there is strong circumstantial evidence but no direct proof.
أهل النصرة (ahl al-nusrah): People of support; those who are capable of providing mutual assistance in a community, typically free adult males. In qasamah, only these are eligible to swear oaths.
أثر (athar): A visible mark or sign on the body indicating violence, such as a wound, bruise, or strangulation mark. Required for qasamah to apply.
السائق والقائد والراكب (al-sā'iq, al-qā'id, al-rākib): The driver (one leading an animal from behind), the leader (one leading from the front), and the rider. These are the handlers of an animal who may be liable for diya if a body is found on the animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is no qasamah or diya required?
A) When the body has a visible wound.
B) When the body shows no mark of violence.
C) When the body is found on an animal.
D) When the residents are less than fifty.

Key Takeaway
Qasamah is not automatic; it requires a clear sign of homicide, and the liability may fall on handlers rather than residents depending on where the body is found.

Will for the Killer and the Heir: Ruling, Dispute, and Heirs' Permission
Ruling on will for killer/heir and effect of heirs' permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wills for the killer and the heir, and explain how the heirs' permission can validate such a will, while also identifying the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the killer's will.

Core Idea
A will for a killer or an heir is void unless the other heirs permit it; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold the killer's will becomes valid with permission, Abu Yusuf disagrees.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses two types of wills that are generally invalid: a will made for the testator's killer, and a will made for an heir. The default rule is that such wills are not enforceable unless the remaining heirs explicitly grant permission after the testator's death. The passage also clarifies that 'killer' means a direct killer, not a cause, and that heirship is determined at death. A significant intra-Hanafi disagreement is discussed: whether the killer's will can be validated by the heirs' permission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three invalid cases
Recognize that the passage states a will is invalid in three situations without permission: (1) exceeding one-third of the estate, (2) for the testator's killer, (3) for an heir. The phrase "إن لم تجز الورثة" applies to all three.
The killer and heir cases are the focus here; the one-third rule is mentioned for context.
2
Define 'killer' and its exceptions
Understand that 'killer' (qatil) means the direct physical killer, not a cause (sabab). Exceptions: if the killer is a minor or insane, the will is valid without permission because they are not liable for punishment. Also, if the killer is the only heir, the will is valid.
The commentary explicitly excludes causes and includes these exceptions.
3
Analyze the prohibition for the heir
The hadith 'لا وصية لوارث' prohibits a will for an heir. Note that heirship is determined at death, not at the time of the will, which affects cases where the heir status changes.
E.g., if a will is made for a brother while he is an heir but later a son is born, the brother is no longer an heir and the will becomes valid. Opposite if heir status changes by death.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on the killer's will
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the killer's will can be validated by the heirs' permission because the prohibition is due to the heirs' right. Abu Yusuf argues it remains invalid even with permission because the prohibition is due to the killer's crime (jinayah).
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
5
Understand conditions for valid permission
Permission (ijazah) must be given after the testator's death by heirs who are adult and sane. Permission given during life can be revoked after death. Partial permission binds only the permitting heir's share.
This distinguishes the timing and binding nature of permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا لقاتله ووارثه إن لم تجز الورثة ويوصى المسلم للذمى، وبالعكس، وقبولها بعد أي ولا تصح إلخ أي وارث الموصى راجع للمسائل الثلاث موته، ويبطل ردّها وقبولها في حياته، وندب النقص من الثلث، أي نقص الوصية بخلاف السبب؛ لأن السبب ليس بقتل حقيقة، فلا يتناوله. واعلم أنه يستثنى من كلامه ما لو كان القاتل صبيا أو مجنونا، فتجوز الوصية لهما بلا إجازة؛ لأنهما ليسا أهلا للعقوبة، أو لم يكن له وارث سوى الموصى له القاتل. وقوله: "ووارثه" أي ولا تصح لوارثه؛ لقوله عليه: "إن الله تعالى أعطى كل ذي حق حقه، فلا وصية لوارث"، ويعتبر كونه وارثا أو غير وارث عند الموت، لا وقت الوصية، حتى لو أوصى لأخيه وهو وارث، ثم ولد له ابن، صحت الوصية للأخ وعكسه لو أوصى لأخيه وله ابن ثم مات الابن قبل موت الموصي بطلت الوصية للأخ. والهبة والصدقة من المريض لوارثه في هذا نظير الوصية، وإقرار المريض لوارثه على عكسه، فيعتبر كونه وارثا أو غير وارث عند الإقرار له. (عيني، فتح) إن لم تجز الورثة راجع إلى الثلاثة المذكورة أي الوصية بما زاد على الثلث وللقاتل والوارث لا تصح إن لم تجز الورثة، وإن أجازوا صحت وقال أبو يوسف: لا تجوز الوصية للقاتل بإجازة الورثة؛ لأن امتناعها للجناية، وهي باقية، ولهما: أن امتناعها لحق الورثة؛ لأن نفع بطلانها يعود إليهم، فتجوز بإجازتهم، ويشترط أن يكون المجيز من أهل التبرع بأن يكون بالغا عاقلا، وإن أجاز البعض يجوز على المجيز بقدر حصته دون غيره لولايته على نفسه لا على غيره، ولا معتبر بإجازتهم حال حياة الموصي ، فلهم أن يرجعوا بعد موت الموصي، ويردوا تلك الإجازة؛ لأنها وقعت ساقطة بخلاف ما إذا أجازوا بعد موته حيث لا يجوز لهم أن يرجعوا عنه؛ لأنها وقعت بعد ثبوت الملك حقيقة، فتلزم. (عيني، تكملة)
And not for his killer and his heir if the heirs do not permit. And the Muslim may make a will for the dhimmi, and vice versa, and its acceptance after any and it is not valid etc. meaning the heir of the testator refers to the three matters: his death, and its rejection and acceptance during his life become void, and it is recommended to reduce from the third, i.e., reduction of the will. Unlike the cause because the cause is not actual killing, so it does not include it. And know that an exception from his statement is if the killer is a child or insane, so the will for them is valid without permission because they are not liable for punishment, or if the testator has no heir other than the killer beneficiary. And his statement: "and his heir" means it is not valid for an heir, due to his statement (peace be upon him): "Indeed Allah, the Exalted, has given every right-holder his right, so no will for an heir." And being an heir or non-heir is considered at the time of death, not at the time of the will. So if he made a will for his brother while he is an heir, then a son is born to him, the will for the brother is valid. And the opposite: if he made a will for his brother while he has a son, then the son dies before the testator's death, the will for the brother becomes void. And the gift and charity from a sick person to his heir in this is analogous to the will, and the sick person's acknowledgment for his heir is the opposite, so being an heir or non-heir is considered at the time of acknowledgment. ('Ayni, Fath) "If the heirs do not permit" refers to the three mentioned: the will exceeding one-third, for the killer, and for the heir are not valid if the heirs do not permit, but if they permit it becomes valid. And Abu Yusuf said: The will for the killer is not valid even with the heirs' permission because its prohibition is due to the crime, which remains. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): Its prohibition is due to the right of the heirs because the benefit of its nullification returns to them, so it becomes valid with their permission. And the condition is that the one permitting is capable of donating, i.e., adult and sane. And if some permit, it is valid for the one who permitted to the extent of his share but not for others, because his authority is over himself not over others. And their permission during the testator's life is not considered, so they may revoke after the testator's death and retract that permission because it occurred void. Unlike if they permitted after his death, where they may not retract because it occurred after the actual establishment of ownership, so it is binding. ('Ayni, Takmilah)
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage is a combination of ruling and commentary on the will for the killer and the heir. The base text states that a will is not valid for the testator's killer, nor for an heir, unless the other heirs permit it. The commentary clarifies that the killer here refers to a direct killer (qatil), not a cause (sabab). Exceptions are made for minors and insane killers, and when the killer is the only heir. The prohibition for an heir is based on the hadith 'No will for an heir.' The status of heirship is determined at death, not at the time of the will. The passage also discusses the intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the will for the killer can be validated by the heirs' permission, while Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that the prohibition is due to the killer's crime, which cannot be waived by the heirs. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as the commentary indicates. Furthermore, the heirs' permission is only effective after the testator's death; permission given during life can be revoked later.

Key Terms
القاتل (al-qatil): The direct killer of the testator, not the cause of death.
الوارث (al-warith): The legal heir at the time of death, not at the time of the will.
الإجازة (al-ijazah): The permission granted by heirs after the testator's death to validate an otherwise invalid will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is the status of being an heir considered for the validity of a will?
A) At the time the will is made
B) At the time of the testator's death
C) At the time of acceptance of the will
D) At the time of distribution of the estate

Key Takeaway
A will for a killer or an heir is invalid unless the other heirs permit after death; permission is binding only if given after death and by capable heirs.

Mutual Validity of Will Between Muslim and Dhimmi
Ruling with proof on mutual will validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wills between Muslims and dhimmis and explain the proofs from the Quran and the contract of dhimmah.

Core Idea
A Muslim and a dhimmi may will to each other because of Quranic permission and transactional equality.

The text establishes that a Muslim may make a will for a dhimmi and vice versa. This reflects the principle that dhimmis are treated equally in financial transactions due to their covenant. The commentary provides two distinct proofs: a Quranic verse (60:8) for the former and the legal equality of dhimmah for the latter. Both directions are valid by consensus within the Hanafi school and beyond.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two directions of will
The passage rules on two scenarios: a Muslim willing to a dhimmi, and a dhimmi willing to a Muslim. Both are pronounced valid.
2
Locate the proof for Muslim→dhimmi
The author cites Quran 60:8 which permits being righteous (birr) to those non-Muslims who do not fight Muslims. This includes making a will as an act of goodness.
The verse is general but is understood to apply to dhimmis.
3
Locate the proof for dhimmi→Muslim
The dhimmi's contract of dhimmah places him equal to Muslims in transactions. Since a dhimmi can donate to a Muslim during life, he can also do so after death via a will.
This reasoning is based on qiyas: donation in life is analogous to donation after death.

Source Text

ويوصي المسلم إلخ: أي تصح وصية المسلم للذمي وبالعكس أي وتصح وصية الذمي للمسلم، أما الأول، فلقوله تعالى: ﴿لا يَنْهَاكُمُ اللَّهُ عَنِ الَّذِينَ لَمْ يُقَاتِلُوكُمْ فِي الدِّينِ وَلَمْ يُخْرِجُوكُمْ مِنْ دِيَارِكُمْ أَنْ تَبَرُّوهُمْ﴾ (الممتحنة: ٨) إلخ وأما الثاني، فلأنه بعقد الذمة ساوى المسلم في المعاملات، حتى جاز التبرع من الجانبين في الحياة، وكذا بعد المماة، وهذان بالإجماع، ولا تصح الوصية للحربي عندنا خلافا للثلاثة. (عيني، فتح)
And the Muslim may make a will etc.: i.e., the will of a Muslim for a dhimmi is valid, and vice versa, i.e., the will of a dhimmi for a Muslim is valid. As for the first, due to His statement: 'Allah does not forbid you from those who do not fight you in religion and do not expel you from your homes that you be righteous toward them' (al-Mumtahanah: 8) etc. As for the second, because through the contract of dhimmah he equals the Muslim in transactions, so donation is permissible from both sides in life, and likewise after death, and these two are by consensus. And the will for a harbi is not valid according to us, contrary to the three (other schools). ('Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the validity of wills between Muslims and dhimmis. The base text states that a Muslim may will to a dhimmi and vice versa. The commentary provides the evidence: for a Muslim willing to a dhimmi, the Quranic verse (60:8) permits being righteous to peaceful non-Muslims. For a dhimmi willing to a Muslim, the contract of dhimmah places him on par with Muslims in transactions, allowing donations in life and after death. These two cases are by consensus (ijma). The text then adds that a will for a harbi (enemy non-Muslim) is invalid according to the Hanafi school, contrary to the three other imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali). This is an inter-madhhab dispute: the Hanafis restrict the verse to dhimmis only, while others include harbis under the general permissibility of goodness.

Key Terms
الذمي (al-dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under Islamic rule with a covenant of protection (dhimmah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, a will made by a dhimmi for a Muslim is:
A) Invalid, because a dhimmi cannot benefit a Muslim after death.
B) Valid, because the contract of dhimmah equates him with Muslims in transactions.
C) Valid only if the dhimmi converts to Islam before death.
D) Invalid, because the Quranic verse only addresses Muslims.

Key Takeaway
Wills between Muslims and dhimmis are valid in both directions by Quranic and contractual proofs, and this is by consensus.

Invalidity of Will for a Harbi: Hanafi Position and Dispute
Inter-madhhab dispute on will for harbi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on wills for harbis and compare it with the positions of the other three schools.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school forbids a will for a harbi, while other schools allow it.

The passage concludes with a point of difference: a will for a harbi (enemy non-Muslim) is invalid according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the three other imams. The Hanafi proof restricts the general permission of Quran 60:8 to those who are not hostile. This disagreement has practical consequences for Muslims living in conflict zones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for harbi
The text states: 'the will for a harbi is not valid according to us' — i.e., in the Hanafi school, a will for a harbi is invalid (batil).
A harbi is a non-Muslim who is not under Islamic protection and is at war with Muslims.
2
State the opposing view
The other three schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that a will for a harbi is valid, based on the general permission of the verse.
The text says 'khilafan lil-thalathah' — contrary to the three.
3
Analyse the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi school limits the verse's application to those who do not fight Muslims (i.e., dhimmis and those under truce). Since a harbi is in a state of war, the verse does not apply.
Additionally, there is no contract of protection (dhimmah) to establish transactional equality.
4
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In the Hanafi school, a harbi cannot receive a bequest unless he becomes a dhimmi. In the other schools, he can receive it without restriction.
This difference applies to today's context: a Muslim cannot will to a citizen of a hostile state that is at war with Muslims.

Source Text

(عيني، فتح)

الحربي (al-harbi): A non-Muslim who is not under Islamic rule and is in a state of war with Muslims.

A) Valid like a will for a dhimmi
B) Invalid
C) Valid only if the harbi becomes a dhimmi before accepting
D) Valid with the permission of the heirs

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits wills to harbis based on the restricted application of Quran 60:8 and the lack of a protective covenant.

8 min
Acceptance After Death Valid Despite Prior Rejection
Ruling on acceptance of will after testator's death with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on acceptance of a will after the testator's death and explain why prior rejection does not invalidate it.

Core Idea
Acceptance is only valid after death; pre-death rejection does not void the will.

This passage clarifies a key rule in Hanafi inheritance law: a beneficiary's acceptance of a will is only effective after the testator's death. Any rejection or acceptance during the testator's life is irrelevant. This lesson examines the evidence and the dissenting opinion of Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The act is the beneficiary's acceptance of a will. Determine the general ruling: acceptance is required for ownership, but it is only considered after death.
The ruling covers timing of acceptance, not the will itself.
2
State the Hanafi position
According to the majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), if a beneficiary says 'I do not accept' during the testator's life, that rejection is not considered. He may accept after death and the acceptance is valid.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Identify the opposing view (Zufar)
Zufar holds that rejection during life voids the will, so no acceptance after death is possible. Understand his analogy: like an acknowledgment voided by denial.
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The response: a will is a contingent transfer (tamlik mudaf) attached to after death. Therefore, rejection before death is irrelevant just as acceptance before death is. The key is that ownership only transfers after death.
The text also notes a stylistic point about the author's wording.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Imagine a testator bequeaths his car to a friend. The friend says 'I don't want it' while the testator is alive. After the testator's death, the friend says 'I accept.' According to Hanafi, the acceptance is valid and he owns the car.
This illustrates the practical effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقبولها بعد موته أي قبول الوصية يعتبر بعد موت الموصي، ولا يعتبر ردها، وقبولها في حال الحياة، حتى لو قال في حياة الموصي: لا أقبل، ثم قبل بعد موته صح القبول عندنا، وقال زفر : لا يصح؛ لبطلان الوصية برده، فلا قبوله بعد ذلك، كالإقرار إذا ارتد بتكذيب المقر له لم يصح تصديقه بعد ذلك، قلنا: الوصية برده تمليك مضاف إلى ما بعد الموت فيعتبر الرد والقبول بعده لا قبله كما لا يعتبران قبل عقد الوصية، وكان ينبغي للمصنف أن يقول: "فيبطل ردها" بالفاء. (عيني، فتح، ملا مسكين) وندب النقص إلخ: أي ندب أن يوصي الإنسان بأقل من ثلث المال، سواء كانت الورثة أغنياء أو فقراء؛ لأن في التنقيص صلة القريب، ثم الوصية بأقل من الثلث أولى من تركها إذا كانت الورثة أغنياء أو يستغنون بنصيبهم، وإن كانت الورثة فقراء، ولا يستغنون بما يرثون، فالترك أولى؛ لما فيه من الصلة والصدقة على القريب، وقد قال عليها: "أفضل الصدقة على ذي الرحم الكاشح"؛ ولأن فيه رعاية حق الفقراء والقرابة جميعا. (ملا مسكين، فتح وغيرهما)
And its acceptance after his death: i.e., acceptance of the will is considered after the death of the testator, and its rejection and acceptance during life are not considered. So if he said during the testator's life: 'I do not accept,' then he accepted after his death, the acceptance is valid according to us. Zufar said: It is not valid, because the will becomes void by his rejection, so no acceptance after that, like an acknowledgment if it is rejected by the denial of the person in whose favor it was made, his later confirmation is not valid. We say: The will is a transfer of ownership attached to after death, so rejection and acceptance are considered after it, not before it, just as they are not considered before the contract of the will. And it would have been better for the author to say: 'so its rejection becomes void' with the letter fa. ('Ayni, Fath, Mulla Miskin) And recommendation of reduction etc.: i.e., it is recommended that a person will less than one-third of the wealth, whether the heirs are rich or poor, because in reducing there is connection with the relative. Then the will with less than one-third is better than leaving it if the heirs are rich or are independent with their shares. And if the heirs are poor and not independent with what they inherit, then leaving it is better, because it contains connection and charity to the relative, and he (peace be upon him) said: 'The best charity is to the harsh relative.' And because it involves considering the rights of the poor and the relatives together. (Mulla Miskin, Fath, etc.)
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage covers two distinct rulings. First, acceptance of a will: the Hanafi school considers acceptance only after the testator's death. If the beneficiary rejects during the testator's life, he may still accept after death. Zufar disagrees, arguing that rejection voids the will. The Hanafi response is that a will is a contingent transfer effective only after death, so pre-death actions don't affect it. Second, the sunnah of willing less than one-third: it is recommended (mandub) to will less than one-third, regardless of the heirs' wealth. If heirs are rich, willing less than one-third is better than no will; if heirs are poor, then leaving the bequest (i.e., not willing at all) is better, because charity to poor relatives is more virtuous. The hadith about preferring harsh relatives supports this.

Key Terms
القبول (al-qabul): The beneficiary's acceptance of the will after the testator's death, which is required for ownership.
الوصية (al-wasiyyah): A bequest or will; a transfer of ownership contingent upon death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a beneficiary says 'I do not accept this will' during the testator's life, then accepts after his death:
A) The acceptance is invalid because the will was already voided
B) The acceptance is valid
C) The will becomes void, but a new will is needed
D) The acceptance is valid only if the testator knew of the rejection

Key Takeaway
Acceptance of a will is only effective after the testator's death; prior rejection does not void the will.

6 min
Sunnah of Willing Less Than One-Third
Recommendation (mandub) to will less than one-third

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi recommendation to will less than one-third and distinguish when leaving a will is better than not.

Core Idea
Willing less than one-third is recommended; if heirs are poor, leaving no will is better.

This lesson discusses the recommendation (mandub) to limit a bequest to less than one-third of the estate. The text explains the considerations based on the heirs' financial status and the virtue of charity to harsh relatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal classification
What is the act? Making a will for less than one-third of the estate. Its classification: recommended (mandub). Understand that mandub is between sunnah and mubah; performing it earns reward, omitting it is not sinful.
The text uses 'nadb' which is synonymous with mandub.
2
State the ruling for different heir situations
The recommendation applies regardless of whether heirs are rich or poor. But the text gives a distinction: if heirs are rich or independent, willing less than one-third is better than not willing at all. If heirs are poor, withholding the will (i.e., not bequeathing) is better because charity to poor relatives is more virtuous.
This is a nuanced ruling based on fiqh al-awlawiyyat (priorities).
3
Note the evidence
The hadith: 'The best charity is to the harsh relative.' This supports preferring charity to needy relatives over bequests to non-relatives or rich heirs.
The hadith is cited in the commentary.
4
Apply to a scenario
Example: A testator has $100,000 and wishes to bequeath $30,000 to a rich friend. The heirs are poor. According to the recommendation, it is better to leave no will so the poor heirs inherit the full amount, as giving to them is charity.
This shows practical application of the priority.
5
Distinguish this ruling
This is a recommendation, not a requirement. One may still will up to one-third, but the sunnah is to will less. The wisdom is to preserve more for the heirs and maintain family bonds.
The one-third limit is a separate legal restriction.

According to the Hanafi recommendation on wills, when is it better to leave no will?
A) When the heirs are rich

8 min
Ownership by Acceptance in Wills and the Istihsan Exception
Ruling that ownership is established by acceptance, with exception if beneficiary dies before accepting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that ownership in a will is contingent on acceptance, explain the exception when the beneficiary dies before acceptance, and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
Acceptance vests ownership in a will; istihsan preserves the bequest if the beneficiary dies before accepting.

This lesson examines a key distinction in Hanafi will (wasiyah) law: while ownership generally requires the beneficiary's acceptance after the testator's death, a special exception arises by juristic preference (istihsan) if the beneficiary dies before accepting. We will unpack the reasoning, contrast it with the Shafi'i position, and see how the Hanafi school balances contractual transfer with the nature of succession.

Guided Path
1
State the base ruling
Identify the rule: the beneficiary owns the bequest by acceptance after the testator's death. Rejection voids it.
Acceptance can be explicit or implied (e.g., taking possession).
2
Identify the exception
Note the exception: if the beneficiary dies before accepting, the bequest passes to his heirs by istihsan, despite no explicit acceptance.
This overrides the general rule.
3
Compare with the Shafi'i view
Understand that the Shafi'i school (and Zufar in one narration) holds that acceptance is not required, likening a will to inheritance.
The Hanafi school distinguishes a will as a contractual transfer.
4
Analyse the istihsan reasoning
The qiyas would void the will (like a sale where buyer dies before acceptance). Istihsan is applied because the testator's side is complete, and the delay is only for the beneficiary's right.
Analogous to a sale with a condition of option for the buyer.
5
Apply to a scenario
Practice: If a testator bequeaths his car to a friend, and the friend dies after the testator but before accepting, the car belongs to the friend's heirs.
This illustrates the practical outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وملك بقبوله : أي ملك الموصى له الوصية بقبوله فإن قبل الموصى له الوصية بعد موت الموصي ثبت الملك له في الموصى به قبضه أو لم يقبضه وإن رد الموصى له الوصية بطلت وصيته برده عندنا، وقال زفر في رواية والشافعي: يملك بدون القبول، ولا تبطل برده؛ لأنها خلافة فلا يحتاج فيها إلى القبول كالميراث. ولنا: أنه تمليك بعقد، فيتوقف على القبول كالتمليك بالهبة والتحقيق في هذا المقام أن قبول الموصى له بالوصية ليس بشرط لصحة الوصية، وإنما هو شرط إفادة الملك للموصى له حيث لا يثبت الملك بالوصية قبل القبول؛ لأنها تشبه الإرث من وجه من حيث إنها تملك بالموت وتشبه الهبة من وجه من حيث إنها تملك بتمليك الغير، ولهذا تبطل الوصية بالخمر، وإن كان الخمر يورث فاعتبرناها بالهبة في حق القبول مادام المقبول موهوبا من الموصى له، فقلنا: بأنها لا تملك قبل القبول واعتبرناها بالإرث بعد القبول، فقلنا: إن الموصى له يملكها بعد القبول من غير قبض عملا بالشبهين بقدر الإمكان. (عيني، ملا مسكين) إلا أن يموت إلخ استثناء من قوله وملك بقبوله أي يملك الموصى له بقبوله، إلا أن يموت الموصى له بعد موت الموصي قبل قبوله الوصية، فإن الموصى له يملك الموصى به بدون القبول، يعني يدخل في ملك ورثة الموصى له استحسانا، والقياس أن تبطل؛ لما بينا أن الملك موقوف على القبول، فصار كموت المشتري قبل قبوله بعد إيجاب البائع، وجه الاستحسان أن الوصية من جانب الموصى قد تمت تماما لا يلحقه الفسخ من جهته، وإنما توقف لحق الموصى له فإذا مات دخل في ملكه، كما في البيع المشروط فيه الخيار للمشتري إذا مات قبل الإجازة. (عيني، فتح)
And ownership by his acceptance: i.e., the beneficiary owns the bequest by his acceptance. So if the beneficiary accepts the will after the testator's death, ownership is established for him in the bequeathed item, whether he takes possession or not. And if the beneficiary rejects the will, his bequest becomes void by his rejection according to us. Zufar in one narration and al-Shafi'i said: He owns without acceptance, and it does not become void by rejection, because it is a succession (khilafah) so it does not need acceptance, like inheritance. Our proof: it is a transfer of ownership by contract, so it depends on acceptance like transfer by gift. The verification in this context is that the beneficiary's acceptance of the will is not a condition for the validity of the will, but it is a condition for conferring ownership to the beneficiary, because ownership is not established by the will before acceptance. This is because it resembles inheritance in one respect (ownership by death) and resembles a gift in another (ownership by transfer from another). For this reason, a will for wine is void, even though wine is inheritable; we considered it as a gift regarding acceptance as long as the beneficiary is the recipient, so we said it is not owned before acceptance. And after acceptance, we considered it as inheritance, so we said the beneficiary owns it after acceptance without taking possession, acting on the two resemblances as much as possible. ('Ayni, Mulla Miskin) Except if he dies etc.: This is an exception from his statement: 'and ownership by his acceptance,' i.e., the beneficiary owns by his acceptance, except if the beneficiary dies after the death of the testator before he accepts the will, then the beneficiary owns the bequest without acceptance, meaning it enters into the ownership of the beneficiary's heirs by istihsan. The analogy (qiyas) would be that it becomes void, as we explained that ownership is contingent on acceptance, so it becomes like the buyer's death before acceptance after the seller's offer. The reason for istihsan is that the will from the testator's side is completely finalized, not subject to revocation from his side; the delay is only for the beneficiary's right, so if he dies, it enters into his ownership, like a sale with a condition of option for the buyer if he dies before ratification. ('Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage discusses the ownership of a bequest. The base ruling is that the beneficiary owns the bequest upon acceptance after the testator's death. Rejection voids it. Zufar and al-Shafi'i differ, arguing that a will is like inheritance and does not require acceptance. The Hanafi response is that a will is a contractual transfer, so acceptance is necessary for ownership, though not for the validity of the will itself. The commentary then distinguishes the will from inheritance and gift: it shares aspects of both. An exception is made if the beneficiary dies before accepting: by istihsan, the bequest becomes part of the beneficiary's estate and passes to his heirs, despite the lack of explicit acceptance. This is based on the idea that the will is complete from the testator's side, so death of the beneficiary should not nullify it. This is analogous to a sale with an option: if the buyer dies before exercising the option, the sale is final.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference: setting aside analogy in favor of a more equitable ruling.
التمليك (al-tamlik): Transfer of ownership, which in wills is contingent on acceptance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if the beneficiary dies after the testator's death but before accepting the will, what happens to the bequest?
A) It becomes void and returns to the testator's estate
B) It passes to the beneficiary's heirs without acceptance (istihsan)
C) It is given to charity
D) It is divided among the testator's heirs

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a will requires acceptance for ownership, but if the beneficiary dies before accepting, istihsan preserves the bequest for his heirs.

Invalid Wills: Debtor, Minor, and Mukatab
Rulings on invalid wills: debtor with overwhelming debt, minor, and mukatab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three categories of persons whose wills are invalid in the Hanafi school and explain the legal reasoning and conditions for each.

Core Idea
A will is a donation (tabarru'), so it requires full legal capacity and solvency.

In Hanafi law, a valid will requires that the testator be adult, sane, free, and solvent. This passage examines three exceptions where the will is invalid despite otherwise meeting formalities: a debtor whose debts exceed the estate, a minor (regardless of age), and a mukatab slave. Each case illustrates how the nature of a will as a non-obligatory donation interacts with legal capacity and prior claims. We'll unpack the reasoning and the inter-madhhab dispute on the minor's will.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three cases
The passage explicitly states that the will is invalid for: (1) a debtor whose debt surrounds the estate, (2) a minor, and (3) a mukatab. Note the condition for the debtor: the will becomes valid if creditors discharge him.
The debtor's case is conditional; the other two are absolute.
2
Understand the reasoning for the debtor
The text says 'because the debt is given precedence; it is a loan, and the will is a donation for a non-obligatory act.' Debts are obligatory and take priority over voluntary bequests. The will is therefore void unless creditors waive their right.
This aligns with the Quranic principle of debt priority (4:11-12).
3
Understand the reasoning for the minor
The Hanafi proof: a will is a donation (tabarru'), and a minor lacks legal capacity for donation. The text refutes the Shafi'i view by emphasizing that even if the minor's will is for charity, he is not qualified. Reward for leaving inheritance suffices.
Note the cross-madhhab difference: Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali permit charitable wills from minors.
4
Understand the reasoning for the mukatab
A mukatab is a slave under contract for emancipation. He has limited ownership; his current assets are not fully owned. Hence a will for a specific item from his current property is invalid by consensus. However, a will contingent on future emancipation (e.g., 'when I am freed') is valid by consensus.
The third division of the mukatab's will is cut off in the text; it likely involves a condition like 'if I die before emancipation'.
5
Synthesise the common principle
All three cases share the flaw that the testator lacks either full ownership (debtor/mukatab) or legal capacity (minor). The will is a gratuitous disposition requiring both.
This principle can be applied to analogous cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تصح وصية إلخ لأن الدين مقدم؛ لكونه قرضا والوصية بغير الواجب تبرع، وحق الشرع من الصلاة ونحوها يسقط بالموت، فتكون الوصية كالتبرع، إلا أن يبرئه الغرماء، فحينئذ تصح. (فتح، ملا مسكين) والصبي: أي لا تصح وصية الصبي أيضا، وقال الشافعي تصح وصيته إذا كانت في وجوه البر؛ لأنه نافع في و به قال مالك وأحمد ولنا أنه تبرع وهو ليس من أهله وما ترك على ورثته يحصل له الثواب، وكذا إذا أوصى، ثم مات بعد الإدراك لم تجز تلك الوصية؛ لعدم الأهلية وقت المباشرة، وكذا إذا قال: إذا أدركت فثلث مالي لفلان . وصية رمز الحقائق: ٤٧٥/٢] والمكاتب أي لا تصح وصية المكاتب أيضا؛ لأنه تبرع، وهو ليس من أهله. واعلم أن وصية المكاتب على ثلاثة أقسام: أحدها باطل بالإجماع، وهو الوصية بعين من أعيان ماله؛ لأنه لا ملك له حقيقة. والثاني: يجوز بالإجماع، وهو ما إذا أضاف الوصية إلى ما يملكه بعد العتق بأن قال إذا أعتقت فثلث مالي وصية لفلان، حتى لو عتق =
And the will is not valid etc. because the debt is given precedence; since it is a loan, and the will is a donation for a non-obligatory act, and the right of the Shari'ah (like prayer etc.) falls away at death, so the will becomes like a donation, unless the creditors discharge him, then it becomes valid. (Fath, Mulla Miskin) And the minor: i.e., the will of a minor is also not valid. Al-Shafi'i said his will is valid if it is for charitable purposes, because it is beneficial, and Malik and Ahmad said the same. Our proof: it is a donation, and he is not qualified for it, and what he leaves for his heirs, he obtains reward. Similarly, if he made a will then died after reaching maturity, that will is not valid due to lack of capacity at the time of performance. Likewise, if he said: 'When I reach maturity, one-third of my wealth is for so-and-so' (as a will). (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/475) And the mukatab: i.e., the will of a mukatab is also not valid because it is a donation and he is not qualified for it. And know that the will of a mukatab has three divisions: one is invalid by consensus, which is the will for a specific item from his assets because he does not have real ownership. The second is permissible by consensus, which is if he affiliates the will to what he will own after emancipation, e.g., he says: 'When I am freed, one-third of my wealth is a will for so-and-so,' so that if he is freed...
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage covers three cases where a will is invalid: (1) A debtor whose debt surrounds his entire estate (i.e., debts exceed assets) - such a person's will is invalid because debts take precedence. However, if the creditors forgive the debt or release the estate, the will becomes valid. (2) A minor (sabi) - his will is invalid because he lacks legal capacity for donation. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, allowing it for charitable purposes. The Hanafi proof is that the minor is not qualified for tabarru' (donation). Even if he makes a will before maturity and dies after, it is invalid. (3) A mukatab (a slave under a contract of manumission) - his will is also invalid because he has limited ownership. The commentary further divides the mukatab's will into three categories: (a) invalid by consensus: will for a specific item from his current assets, as he does not truly own them; (b) permissible by consensus: will contingent on future emancipation (e.g., 'when I am freed'); (c) (the third division is incomplete in the text, but typically it relates to a conditional will). The passage also references Ramz al-Haqa'iq for the minor's will ruling.

Key Terms
المديون (al-madyun): A debtor whose debts surround (i.e., exceed or equal) the entire estate.
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has made a contract with his master to purchase his freedom by paying installments.
تبرع (tabarru'): A voluntary donation or gratuitous act; a will is classified as tabarru'.
الغرماء (al-ghurama'): Creditors; they have the right to priority over bequests until debts are paid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) If the will is for a charitable cause
B) If the creditors discharge him from the debt
C) If the will does not exceed one-third
D) If the heirs permit

Key Takeaway
A will is valid only if the testator is an adult, sane, free, and solvent; debtors with overwhelming debt, minors, and mukatabs lack one of these conditions, making their wills invalid (with specific exceptions).
